this Dictionary of Kiikaonde …Owes its existence to the following things: A disambiguation of a partially corrupted OCR scan of the Kiikaonde English Vocabulary compiled in 2004 by D. B. Wright. (Provided by Mr. Mung’anga of Kamabuta) The Peace Corps RED (Rural Education Development) Kiikaonde training manual made by Steven? and Gershom K…? and others? My friends and neighbors in the Mufumbwe area (name them) Things we’ll hopefully be fixing: Accessibility and Organization The accessibility issue is two-fold. The first (fold) is that previous vocabularies of Kiikaonde tend to under-explain both linguistic terminology and vocabulary items, which just makes it needlessly difficult for a lay reader trying to learn the language. (Completely understandable if you’re trying to save space in a printed dictionary, but WELCOME TO THE CYBERVERSE, BABY) Second, that these resources simply don’t exist online or in most (any?) libraries. D. B.’s Kiikaonde English Vocabulary (2004) isn’t even mentioned online anywhere. I had to get it from a guy! Like, as in: not an institution, like the call number of this fucking book is “Go to rural Northwest Zambia and find Mr. Mung’anga’s house and he’s got one copy of this book that he printed himself because it simply doesn’t exist anywhere meaningfully in the public sphere. Second: the organization. The organization of past dictionaries has also been constrained by physicality in a way that can hopefully be avoided here (cf. in the fucking cyberverse, baby) hopefully without causing any… new problems. Physical dictionaries tend to be organized alphabetically, but alphabetization makes a little less sense in a language powered by agreement and prefixes. (For example, with D. B. et al.’s 2004 publication, it’s a little disorienting to have all the demonstratives mixed up in the larger vocabulary. Demonstratives and like “here” and “there”s would be better served by a chart, because really you can think of it as the same word with different agreements.) D. B. et al.’s work is a major win in many respects, considering that J. L. Wright’s 1983? dictionary fell to what I consider the pitfall of organizing the dictionary alphabetically by English word. “A. there is no indefinite article in the kaonde language.” Hello??? We’ll also be taking a line from the Tonga dictionary () (and others?) and seperating the verbs and nouns. I’ll be going a little further and making separate sections for demonstratives, pronouns, etc. and also (maybe) organizing the nouns by their noun class. It seems unreasonable that this isn’t the norm for Bantu languages. (The nouns are already organized! It’s (hahaha) it’s so easy!) Note on colonialism and evangelism Fuck colonialism. Fuck neocolonialism. Fuck extractive capitalism. Fuck evangelism. Fuck empire. Fuck the fact that English being the de facto language of commerce is rooted in imperialism and still exerts soft power over colonized countries. (Okay, that last one is complicated.) There are benefits to knowing multiple languages. English is not a special language. You can go your whole life without comprehending a word of English and have a completely fine, happy life. Many people do.  CONTROL F The organization of this work is predicated on the use of the control F (or command F) feature rather than traditional alphabetical organization. (Also the table of contents, also hyperlinks) If you’re unable to find a word in Kiikaonde, try modifying your search by adding or subtracting vowels. Some words have long vowels that are distinct in meaning from otherwise identical words. (hard for english speakers to hear an initial nasal, like, nshima might be heard as shima. Might also be being pronounced as shima in casual speech. But in any case this wouldn’t affect a control F search, just the placement in the dictionary. That is to say, I’m probably not going to have an entry from Shima that just directs you to nshima.) Regardless of the method by which the dictionary is organized, it might be difficult to find words for a few reasons. Verbs (and other things) are modified by pronouns, nouns (and shit) are modified by agreement. Another big trouble is, as in learning any other language, it’s not easy to pick out individual words especially from rapid speech. (See: we don’t actually talk in words but writing makes us think we do.) (See: at first I kept hearing this word iyakuno! everywhere and it took me a while to realize it was iya kuno — come here! iya is not that far from the infinitive kwiya that I was more used to, but it was far enough to throw me) It’s also helpful to have an understanding of the sound system Kiikaonde uses— PROJECT “HAVE MY CAKE AND EAT IT TOO” B-Side: alphabetical dictionary A-Side: NUMBERS (Arabic numbers are used more frequently for numbers larger than five…?) Preface from the Kiikaonde English Vocabulary Preliminary Edition (2004) Compiled by D. B. Wright. It is not possible to acknowledge all the sources from which the words in this dictionary have been obtained. Our grateful thanks to the very many who have produced words hitherto unknown to us, and have spent many hours patiently discussing, checking, explaining and correcting earlier vocabularies. These include Mr. Shaantolo Henry of Solwezi Teacher Training College who has also provided valuable cultural material, Mr. K.C.Muyebaa and other members of the Kiikaonde Language Committee, Mr. Hurbert Kakonkanya, editor, of the Zambia Educational Publishing House, and Miss Doreen Adams, for many years at Mukinge (E.C.Z.), Kasempa. The compiler of the dictionary should have been John L. Wright (Language in Zambia Grammatical Sketches, Institute for African Studies, University of Zambia, 1977). However, his death in 1983 left this work to a less able compiler, who has endeavoured to provide a fitting tribute to his memory. — D.B. Wright November 1994 The following have been mentioned by name or initials in the text: 'Dictionary of the Kaonde Language' by Broughall Woods (an early: Colonial Government Officer in the Kaonde - Lunda area) - published by R.T.S. in 1924. I am grateful to the United Society for Christian Literature, the present holders of the copyright, for permission to quote from this work. Entries used have been checked by modern Kaonde speakers and the orthography updated. Mainly quoted for interesting cultural notes. Rev. Charles S. Foster, missionary in the Kasempa area from 1917, and responsible for Kiikaonde language teaching for fifty years. Much of the vocabulary is to be found in his word lists, but he is only quoted specifically when he differs from others in meaning. Chibanza - the late Simon; Chief Chibanza of Solwezi District. Words found in his book “Bufumu bwa bu Kasempa”. Ansell - W.F.H. Ansell, Ranger in the Game and Tsetse Control Dept. Solwezi District 1947 Quoted re. identity of animals. Forestry Dept. of GRZ - (A.E.G. Storrs) book 'Know Your Trees', published by the Forestry Department, Ndola, 1979. Fanshawe (Principal Scientific Officer, Division of Forest Research) - books 'Fifty Common Trees of Zambia' - GRZ 19472.   ABBREVIATIONS etc. borrowed word (source given where known) adj. adjective adv. adverb affirm. affirmative anat. anatomical arch. archaic, old Bib, Bible, biblical cf. compare conj. conjunction demonstr demonstrative educ. educational e.g. for example exclam. exclamation fig. figurative, figuratively freq. frequently gram. grammar, grammatical ideo. ideophone, onomonotopea () a word meant to sound like the thing it’s describing. It’s usually derived from a word that means the same thing. In English, “achoo!” is an ideophone for sneezing. In Kiikaonde, “chamu” is derived from “kuchamuna” (to sneeze). i.e. that is, in other words inten. interjection ling. linguistics lit. literal, literally med. medical N.T. Edition 1984) BZ = Birds of Zambia WORD FORMS Nouns are normally shown in their singular form. If the plural is the more usual form, the singular will also be given. When the plural of nouns is normal (see following table) it is not usually given; but when there is a choice of prefix, or when the plural prefix is irregular, or the singular prefix is missing (e.g. bookwe) or it is a"foreign' word, the plural prefix is shown. The regular, singular and plural prefixes of the noun classes are as follows: Singular — Plural MU (personal) — BA (also honorific) MU (impersonal - trees, some animals etc.) — MI KI — BI KA — TU LU — N BU (abstract) — (abstracts have no plural) MA — LU?????? MU/MI (trees, natural features, animals, and body parts) 5. JI (contains a number of expressions to do with time, e.g. jíngá ‘how often?’ jibiji ‘again’, jisatu ‘thrice’.) 5/6. JI/MA ???? (natural items…) 7. KI (pejorative when prefixed to other noun classes, denotes worthlessness when -e is suffixed.) 7/8. KI/BI (many concrete objects, of languages and of customs or characteristics of particular tribes.) 9/6. N/MA 11. LU (abstract, except for lúya ‘steam’, luvúla ‘wind’, and lúkungu ‘dust’ and others.) 12/13. KA/TU (contains many small things. Prefixing a noun from another class with ka- makes it diminutive. Prefixing an uncountable noun from another class (like salt or water) indicates a small amount of it.) 14. BU (many abstract words) Butemwe, but also buki. 15. KU (verb infinitives) 15/6. KU/MA (four body parts: kuulu/maulu, kutwi/matwi, kwapa/mwapa, kuboko/maboko.) 16. PA (Locative preprefix “at”) 17. KU (Locative preprefix “to”) 18. MU (Locative preprefix “in”) Verbs are shown in their shortest form - the root, followed by the stem of the distant past tense. For this reason, 'to' has been omitted from the English equivalent, except where the English word can also be confused with another part of speech, e.g. a noun, in which case, if the meaning is not clear 'to' has been inserted in brackets - (to). Most Kaonde verbs have many derived forms. Only a few of these have been shown, mainly when the meaning or form is not obviously connected with the simple form of the verb. Infinitive form Pros and Cons +seems common to use ku form +more easily searchable -kampe harder to take away the ku and add b/ya/n/mu/u/wa etc. Base form Pros and Cons +easier to add other prefixes -sounds like strong commands -would have to put a dash infront of every root to be truly accurate Brief grammatical overview, but link to JL NUMBERS JINA adv. - four times. -SATU adj. - numerical suffix, three; n. - trio, threesome. NOTE: makumi ataanu na jimo = 60; makuml ataanu ne mo = 51 PURE ADJECTIVE STEMS These take the adjective concords, e.g. muntu mukwabo ‘another person’. -KAJI adj. - angry, ferocious, fearsome, severe, fierce, wild, brutal, dangerous, savage. -KAZHI - female -lamba be smooth, be beautiful, be pretty -nyike — young -kulumpe — elderly, venerable -ana ‘young’ -bi ‘bad’ -bishi ‘unripe’ -cheche ‘small’ -enyi ‘strange’ -ine ‘self, the very’ -ingi ‘many’ -ipi ‘short’ -katampe ‘big’ -kwabo ‘other, additional’, -lume ‘male’ -ncha ‘worthless’ -pya ‘new’ -tanshi ‘first’ -tuntulu ‘whole’ -umi ‘live’ -ya ‘beautiful’ -yampe ‘beautiful’. -nka — only (bantu bonka ‘only the people’; bunke bwami? only me) -nse — all, every (bintu byonse ‘everything’) The stems ‘-nka ‘only’ and ‘-nse ‘all, every’ take the second-object-concord, e.g.,. A 2 - Concord of plural nouns whose prefix is MA-; also MF-, and some of MO-, where the original MA- prefix elides with the following vowel - e.g. meeso, mooba. A 3 - Used instead of U-' for the subject concord for 1st and 3rd. class nouns in the relative and subjunctive mood e.g. byo aayiile - when he went; atweele - let him enter. MUNO adv., demonstr. adj. or pronoun (in initial or non initial position) - (in) here, this (place) (distant from both speaker and person addressed). VERBS start ABA (-aabile) v.t. - alot, give out. ABAKA (-abamine) v.i. - be immersed. ABANA (-abeene) v.i. - divide, part, be severed or parted. ABANYA (-abeenye) v.t. - divide, separate, part, alienate, make or arrange a division, separate combatants, seclude, disjoint, distribute, ration; kwaabanya pakachi: to halve. ABAUKA (-abaukile) v.i. - talk nonsense, be in delirium, rave. ABIKA (-abikile) v.t. - immerse, soak. ABILA (-abijile) v.t. - alot to, share out to. ABUKA (-abukile) v.t. - ford, cross water (cf. kyaabu: a bridge, stream crossing (ford). ABULA (-abwile) v.t. - take out of water (reversive of ABIKA) ABUSHA (-abushile) v.t. - ferry, take across water etc. AKAMWA 1 (-akaminwe) v.i. - be agitated, or confused with fear, be dumb with apprehension, anxious. AKAMWA 2 (-akaminwe) v.t. - (to) fear with reverence, be devout, revere. n. kwaakamwa - fear, foreboding, piety, reverence;, kwaakamwa Leesa - the fear of the Lord; adj. - bula kwaakamwa Leesa - ungodly. ALA (-ajile) v.t. - lay out (e.g. lay, set table); spread out - mat, blankets etc. (cf. Mwaala kapala - spreader of the firmament - an old name or attribute of God); build nest; kwaala makuunda: to cross the legs. AMWA (-aminwe) v.t. - suck at the breast.?? ALAANANA (-alaanene) v.i. - wallow, grovel, roll over and over, roll in pain, writhe, be convulsed, a. paanshi: prostrate oneself in worship. ??ALAANANYA (-alaanenye) v.t. - roll over. ALAMA 1 (-alamine) v.i. - used for a girl undergoing Kisuungu, before she comes out in the open on the last day, or before a dancing ceremony. ‘waalama uji mu Kisuungu'. Some totems do not practice this. AMWIIBHA see AMUSHA (to suckle) ANA (-aine) v.t. - relate, tell a story (kyaano or lwaano) with sung refrain; (naano - story-telling). ALAMA 2 (-alamine) v.i. - be circumcised) (from Lunda). ALAMIISHA (-alamiishe) v.t. - circumcise.) Because the Bakaonde do not practice circumcision there is no verb for it in Kikaonde, but these Lunda verbs are sometimes used. However -tweezhiwa /-tweezha mu mukaanda is preferred. ANDA (-anjile) v.t. - (colloquial) beat, hit. -anda nkoofi: punch. (from Lunda). ANAMA (anamine) v.i. - bask in the sun (of reptiles). ANGALA (-angeele) v.i. - persist in doing wrong, refuse to listen to reason, be hostile. ANGATA (-angachile) v.t. - confiscate, remove, rob, seize, snatch/ take away. ALUKA (-alukile) v.i. - change, be converted, face about, turn, turn round; -aluka ke... become. Kwaaluka n. change, metamorphosis. ANGULA (-angwiile) v.t. - peel, pick beans, husk. (reversive of anshika) - unlay, unmake, take up ALULUKA (-alulukile) v.i. - turn round (completely), be converted; kwaaluluka n. conversion. ANUNA (-anwiine) v.t. (rev. of anyika) - pick up and bring in something that has been spread out or hung up to dry (e.g. mat on which meal has been spread to dry). Special use: the day after the Kisuungu ceremony, the kamwaale is placed outside on a mat for all to see and contribute money on the mat or a plate. The removing from the mat back into the house is known as 'Kwaanuna' - 'baankeembele baanuna kamwaale pa kuleemba juuba. ALULULA (-alulwiile) v.t. - give a different shape or form, change appearance of, disguise, turn round, steer, reverse (as a car). waalulula n. steersman ALULULWA (-alulwilwe) v.i. - be changed in form etc; adj. koonsha kwaalululwa: capable of being changed in shape ALUMUKA (-alumukile) v.i. - roll over. ALUMUNA (-alumwiine) v.t.- heave over, roll a heavy object over (e.g. use a pole as a lever). ANWE BEENE pron. - yourself (polite), yourselves. ANYIKA (-anyikile) v.t. - spread out in the sun, air, dry in the sun, peg out a skin or hide. AMBA (-amble) v.i. - say, speak, state, remark, talk, recite, relate, utter (syn. peleba). ambatu: talk nonsense, conj. sing. or pl.), saying (that). and aamba, naamba (with 1st person (i.e. introducing direct speech). aamba + subjunct. denotes purpose. APA 3 (-apile) v.t - pick, pluck (fruit, flowers) AMBAULA (-ambawile) v.i. discuss - often at fireplaces (syn. -isamba). cf. -choocheka - (to) debate. BU- 1. prefix and concord of BU- class nouns - often with connotation of state, characteristics, quality; when placed before other nouns it can add this meaning, BU- 2. Prefixed to numeral = ordinal “juuba ja butaanu”- the 5th. day. BU- 3. Prefixed to tribal name = tribal territory e.g. bu surety, BU- 4. BWIILAANDE n. - ‘drug' known to the Luba tribe and sometimes used by the Bakaonde, which, when drunk, causes the drinker to behave like a lion, crawling on all fours and singing. He is called a mwillaande. The dance also is known as bwillaande. The mwillaande dances himself into an ecstasy, and becomes temporarily possessed of great physical endurance, but is apparently not dangerous while under the influence.' (B.W.) There are sexual implications of the dance. BWIILAME n. - politics. BWIILU n. (pl. not heard) - species of edible root. BWIILUBE n. - innocence, ignorance. HOONGE / HOONGWE n. - red lechwe (?from Shinkoya) I Note: the past distant form of reflexive, reciprocal and associative forms of verbs has been shown in the normal way. However the tense particle -a- elides with the initial (prefixed) -i or -li of these verbs in the past distant and perfect tenses, e.g. -ibiikamo - beebiikilemo - they interfered. I 1- short concord of N- class and MI- class nouns (becomes Y before a vowel). (Isakunoka vs. yakunoka - It will soon rain vs. it just rained.) I 2- reflexive pronoun prefixed to the verb root: kutemwa - to love; kwitemwa - to love oneself; such verbs may also have a reciprocal meaning when used in the plural, followed by beene na beene (each other): kutemwa - to love; beetemwa beene na beene - they love each other (syn. temwaanana); also associative used with pamo: e.g. kwiibuumba pamo - to bundle together. I 3 - copula, prefixed to relative pronoun, e.g. iye - it is he/she??, ikyo - that's it, etc. IIBYO interj, - quite so; alright, (ke byo iibyo — right? is that not so?) (mute — the sun) JIIZHINA — name KA1 - subject or object concord of KA- class nouns (pl. tu-) (frequently with diminutive connotation). Can be used as a “free” prefix to other classes to make a diminutive e.g. ka kiintu - a little thing. KA 2 - infix particle denoting of time and space KA 3 - infix particle - lest, for fear that, in case … mwaaka pona - in case you fall. KA 4 - Idiomatic (frequently in narrative) use with subjunctive mood as 'then' or 'now' - kaangye -then I went... (or 'now I'm going') (changes to ke(e) in the plural): keeba ye - 'then they went' or 'now they're going'). KA 5 - Negative particle. KA 6 - a place where there is/are .., a place where something frequently happens - e.g. ka mipolo - a place Where there are often tears - e.g. a grave yard. Usually a separate word Except when part of a proper (e.g village) name e.g. Kamatete - a place where there are many needs. KA 7 Interrogative - what? Kika? What is it mambo ka? - why? i.e. for what reason ) etc. amba ka? — say what? KAAMPATATA n. - loincloth - worn between legs and tucked into belt front and back. KEKE! ideo. for heartburn (kikekula) KE 1 - negative - abbreviation of kechi: ke nayuuka ne - I don't know. 2 - Used like a copula: it is… KI- 1 - prefix and short concord of ??HI- class nouns. pl. bi KI- 2 - In the case of days of the week -ki- preceded by pa forms the ordinal: e.g. pa kina (stress on second syllable) - 4th. day = Thursday. Also in some other cases e.g. wa kibiji - 2nd in command, vice-. KI-3 - The prefix ki- can have the connotation "big, bad’ and is sometimes added to other classes for this purpose, e.g. lu kinyama - large, evil creature. KI-4 - Infix particle immediately preceding a verb: - (a) still, yet; e.g. bakijiko - they are still there. (but 'not yet' = kyaangye). (b) first of all e.g. nkipwiishe - let me first finish. LOONO - destruction LU- prefix of lu- (ma-) and lu- (n-) classes of nouns. Abstract lu- class nouns, plus lukuungu, lubesha, luvula, luya and lufukuchi (and possibly luto) take the concord lu- and have no plural. Lubaji and Lweendo also take lu- concord; their plural forms being mbaji and malweendo respectively. Other lu- class nouns take u-, wa- concords. Most LU- class animals form their plural with BALU-. As some plurals are irregular, all (known) plural prefixes of concrete nouns are shown. The lu- prefix can also be associated with size, or length, and can be prefixed to an existing noun, e.g. lunyama. When combined with ki- (e.g. lu kinyama) has the force of “big, bad” (as the 'evil beast' of Lumweekesho 13:1). Lu- may also replace ki- as the prefix of a descriptive noun when it refers to a number of people or things e.g. mwaanyike uji na kisaangajimbwe, baanyike baaji na lusaangajimbwe. (H.R. — It’s not like a tomato at all. I don’t like them! But it’s better than eating only nshima if both are offered to you; like that behavioral psychology study where participants sat in a boring room with a button that gave you an electric shock and more often opted to hit the shock button instead of doing nothing.) MWAANE voc. - a word added to the ends greetings and other phatic phrases (phrases to initiate conversation, establish a channel of communication, show openness or interest, like: hey! how are you? (“how are you?”) is not really a question, it’s a phatic phrase.) (or used by itself) to increase politeness and show respect. (A: Byepi mwane? — how (are you) (respectful) B: Bulongotu mwane! Kana anweba? — Just fine! And you?) Also, as “mwa?”: “what did you say?” or “go ahead, I’m listening.” MWAANGA n. young man; adj. -a mwaanga - handsome. Iwiigwiizho) NSHIKU n. - tree, African blackwood (Dalberg:: melanoxvion).?? NSHIMA n. nshima. if you don’t know what nshima is, what are you even doing. J.L. defines it as “mush”, but that’s not quite right. It’s a dish made from maize-meal. It’s a necessary component of a meal for a meal to be defined as such — otherwise you’re just eating a snack. Unfortunately nshima is far from being a complete protein (it’s just corn and water), so its centrality may be over-hyped, but it’s very filling. NSHIPILA n. - spitting cobra (-shipa - (to) spit).
